بعض ۔ (مصنف ابن ابی شیبۃ ، باب مارخص فيه من الصدقة علی بنی ھاشم ، ج ثانی ، ص ۴۳۲، نمبر ۱۰۷۳۵) اس اثر میں ہے کہ بنی ہاشم ایک دوسرے کو زکوۃ دے سکتے ہیں ، تو اس پر قیاس کر کے بہت غربت ہو تو دوسروں کی زکوۃ بھی بنی ہاشم کو دے سکتے ہیں۔
Chapter of Disposition: Permissibility of giving it to Banu Hashim. Because these three (Ali, Aqil, Jafar) fully supported the Prophet, and they have a fifth share of khums, and the Prophet's hadith addressed them prohibiting zakat. Since other uncles were not prohibited from zakat, nor their children, it is permissible for them to take zakat. Imam Tahawi said: Nowadays Banu Hashim are not in good condition, so it is permissible for their poor to take zakat. Proof (1): Zakat is the filth of people, and eating filth is not good for the family of the Messenger. (2): That his father Rabi'ah bin Harith and Abbas bin Abdul Muttalib said to Abdul Muttalib bin Rabi'ah and Fadl bin Abbas: Go to the Messenger of Allah... Then the Messenger said to us: These sadaqat are only the filth of people, and they are not permissible for Muhammad nor for the family of Muhammad. (Muslim, chapter on prohibition of zakat upon the Prophet and his family, they are Banu Hashim and Banu Abdul Muttalib, p.345 no. 2482/1072; Tirmidhi, chapter on dislike of sadaqah for the Prophet, his family, and his freed slaves, p.42 no.657). From this hadith it is known that zakat is not permissible for Muhammad and the family of Allah. (3): Zakat is not permissible for the children of Ali himself. Its proof is this hadith: I heard Abu Hurairah say: Hasan bin Ali took a date from the dates of sadaqah and put it in his mouth. The Prophet said 'Kakh kakh' to make him throw it, then said: Did you not know that we do not eat sadaqah? (Bukhari, chapter on what is mentioned about sadaqah for the Prophet and his family, p.242 no.1490). In this hadith, Hasan, the son of Ali, was forbidden from eating the dates of zakat. (4): Zakat is not permissible for the freed slave of Banu Hashim. For this is the hadith: From Abu Rafi' that the Prophet sent a man from Banu Makhzum to collect sadaqah... He said: Sadaqah is not permissible for us, and the freed slaves of a people are from themselves. (Tirmidhi, chapter on dislike of sadaqah for the Prophet, his family, and his freed slaves, p.142 no.657; Abu Dawud, chapter on sadaqah upon Banu Hashim, p.245 no.1650). In this hadith it is that zakat is not permissible for the mawla of Banu Hashim. (4) In this time the condition has worsened, and if there is no other way, some honorable muftis have given leeway to give zakat to Banu Hashim. This hadith is about giving sadaqah to a freed slave girl: From Anas that the Prophet was brought meat given as sadaqah to Barirah, and he said: It is sadaqah for her, and for us it is a gift. (Bukhari, chapter on when sadaqah changes, p.202 no.1495). In this hadith it is that his freed slave girl was given zakat. (5) In athar: From Abu Jafar: There is no harm in sadaqah from some of Banu Hashim to others. (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, chapter on what is permitted of sadaqah upon Banu Hashim, vol.2, p.432, no.10735). In this athar it is that Banu Hashim can give zakat to each other, so by analogy, if there is extreme poverty, they can also receive zakat from others.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage from Thamaratun Najah discusses the ruling on giving zakat to Banu Hashim, the clan of the Prophet (peace be upon him). The author opens by stating that giving zakat to Banu Hashim is permissible under certain conditions. He explains that the Prophet's uncles and their families were generally prohibited from receiving zakat, but exceptions exist for those who did not fully support the Prophet in early Islam. The author then cites Imam Tahawi's view that due to current hardship, zakat is permissible for the poor among Banu Hashim. The passage provides multiple proofs: (1) A general principle that zakat is people's filth and thus unsuitable for the Prophet's family; (2) The famous hadith from Sahih Muslim that sadaqat (including zakat) are not permissible for Muhammad and his family, defined as Banu Hashim and Banu Abdul Muttalib; (3) The hadith of Hasan bin Ali being forbidden from taking a date from sadaqah, showing that even the children of Ali are included; (4) The ruling extends to freed slaves (mawali) of Banu Hashim, as they follow the same status; (5) A narration from Abu Jafar that Banu Hashim may give to each other, and by qiyas (analogy), if there is extreme need, they may receive from others. The author also mentions that some later muftis have given leeway in dire circumstances. The overall ruling is that zakat is normally haram for Banu Hashim, but it becomes permissible when they are in extreme poverty and no other source is available.

Key Terms
زکوۃ (zakāh): The obligatory alms tax on wealth, one of the five pillars.
بنی هاشم (Banū Hāshim): The clan of the Prophet Muhammad, including his family and descendants.
آل محمد (Āl Muḥammad): The family of Muhammad, interpreted as Banu Hashim and Banu Abdul Muttalib.
مولی (mawlā): Freed slave or client.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on giving zakat to a poor person from Banu Hashim in normal circumstances?
A) Permissible
B) Recommended
C) Prohibited
D) Obligatory

Key Takeaway
Zakat is normally prohibited for Banu Hashim, but it becomes permissible when they are in extreme poverty and have no other means.

Zakat — Almsgiving
beginner
6 min
Prohibited Zakat Recipients: Family and Slaves
Lists those to whom zakat cannot be given due to dependency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the categories of people to whom zakat cannot be given according to the Hanafi school and explain the underlying principle of dependency.

Core Idea
Zakat cannot be given to anyone whose maintenance is your obligation — it would return to you.

This passage from Thamaratun Najah identifies specific categories of people to whom paying zakat is invalid. The Hanafi school bases this on the principle that if the recipient's livelihood is the giver's responsibility, the zakat effectively returns to the giver, failing to fulfill the obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibited categories
The passage lists four categories: one's own ascendants (usul), descendants (furu'), spouse, and slaves (including mukatib and part-free). Recognize that all share the characteristic of being dependents.
Usul includes parents and grandparents; furu' includes children and grandchildren.
2
Understand the dependency principle
The core reasoning is that if the giver is obligated to support the recipient, giving zakat to them is like spending on oneself. Therefore, the zakat obligation is not fulfilled.
Nafaqah (maintenance) is wajib for these relatives.
3
Examine the textual proofs
The author cites two athar: one from Ali (ra) that parents and children have no right in obligatory sadaqah, and one from Ibn Abbas (ra) that zakat should not go to dependents.
These are mursal hadith, but accepted in the Hanafi school.
4
Apply the ruling to edge cases
Note the distinction between one's own slave (invalid) and another's mukatib (valid because the benefit does not return to the giver). This reinforces the principle.
Giving to one's own mukatib is invalid because the money ultimately benefits the owner.

Source Text

باب المصرف
(١١٩٦) واصل المزكي وفرعه (۱۱۹۷) وزوجته (۱۱۹۸) ومملوكه ومكاتبه ومعتق بعضه
ترجمه : (۱۱۹۶) اور زکوۃ دینے والے کے اصول ( ماں باپ، دادا دادی وغیرہ) اور فروع ( بیٹا بیٹی، پوتا پوتی وغیرہ) تشریح : جو آدمی اصل ہو جیسے باپ، دادا، پر دادا، ماں، دادی، پردادی، نانا، نانی ۔ یا جو آدمی کا فروع ہو، جیسے بیٹا، بیٹی ، پوتا، پوتی ، پر پوتا، پر پوتی، نواسہ، نواسی، پرنواسه، یا پر نواسی ، تو چونکہ ان لوگوں کا نان نفقہ آدمی کے ذمے ہوتا ہے تو ان کو دینا گویا کہ زکوۃ اپنے ہی کے اوپر خرچ کرنا ہوا، اس لئے ان لوگوں کو زکوۃ دینا جائز نہیں ہے۔ وجه (۱) ان لوگوں کے ساتھ اتنا گہرا رابطہ ہوتا ہے کہ ان کا نان و نفقہ بھی اپنے ہی ذمہ ہوتا ہے۔ اس لئے ان لوگوں کو دینا گویا کہ زکوۃ کا مال اپنے ہی پاس رکھ لینا ہے۔ اس لئے زکوۃ کا مال ان لوگوں کو دینے سے زکوۃ کی ادائیگی نہیں ہوگی (۲) عـــن عــلــی بـن طالب : ليس لولد و لا والد حق في صدقة مفروضة ، و من كان له ولد أو والد فلم يصله فهو عاق (سنن بیہقی ، باب لا يعطيها من تلزمه نفقته من ولده ووالديه من تهم الفقراء والمساكين، ج سابع ص ۴۵ نمبر ۱۳۲۲۹) اس اثر میں ہے کہ فروع یعنی اولاد، اور اصول یعنی والد وغیرہ کا حق زکوۃ میں نہیں ہے۔ (۳) اثر میں ہے کہ جن لوگوں کی کفالت کرتا ہو اور اصول و فروع میں سے ہوں ان کو زکوۃ دینے سے زکوۃ کی ادائیگی نہیں ہوگی ۔ عن ابن عباس قال لا بأس ان تجعل زکوتک فی ذوی قرابتک مالم يكونوا في عيالك (مصنف ابن ابي هيئة ۹۶ ما قالوافی الرجل یدفع زکوتہ الی قرابته ج ثانی ص ۴۱۲، نمبر ۱۰۵۳۱ ر مصنف عبدالرزاق ، باب لمن الزكوة ج رابع ص ۸۸ نمبر ۷۱۹۳ ) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ جو قریب کے رشتہ دار ہوں اور اس کی قدرتی طور پر کفالت بھی کرتا ہو تو اس کو زکوۃ دینے سے زکوۃ کی ادائیگی نہیں ہوگی۔
ترجمه : (۱۱۹۷) اور اس کی بیوی کو ۔
تشریح : بیوی شوہر کے قرابت میں ہے اور اس کا نفقہ شوہر پر لازم ہے اس لئے بیوی کو بھی زکوۃ دینا جائز نہیں ہے۔ وجه (۱) عن ابن عباس قال لا بأس ان تجعل زكوتک فی ذوی قرابتک مالم يكونوا في عيالك ) مصنف ابن ابی شیبه ۹۶ ما قالوا فی الرجل یدفع زکوتہ الی قرابته ج ثانی ص ۴۱۲، نمبر ۱۰۵۳۱ ر مصنف عبد الرزاق ، باب لمن الزكوة ج رابع ص ۸۸ نمبر ۷۱۹۳ ) اس اثر میں ہے کہ جو کفالت میں ہو ان کو زکوۃ نہیں دے سکتے۔ اور بیوی شوہر کی کفالت میں ہے اس لئے زکوۃ اس پر ہی لوٹ آئے گی۔
ترجمه : (۱۱۹۸) اور مملوک غلام ، اور مکاتب اور ایسے غلام جس کا کچھ حصہ آزاد ہو ان کو بھی زکوۃ دینا جائز نہیں۔ تشریح : اپنے غلام کو دینا گویا کہ اپنے پاس ہی زکوۃ رکھ لینا ہے اس لئے اپنے غلام کو نہیں دے سکتا۔ دوسرے کے مکاتب کو تو زکوۃ دے سکتا ہے تا کہ وہ مال کتابت ادا کر کے آزادگی حاصل کر لے لیکن اپنے مکاتب غلام کو زکوۃ نہیں دے سکتا، کیونکہ اس زکوۃ کی رقم پھر مالک کی طرف آجائے گی ، تو زکوۃ دینے والے کی زکوۃ واپس اسی کی طرف آگئی اس لئے مکاتب کو مکمل طور پر زکوۃ کا مالک بنانا
Chapter of Disposition: (1196) And the giver's usul (ancestors) and furu' (descendants), (1197) and his wife, (1198) and his slave, his mukatib, and his part-free slave. Translation: (1196) And the giver's usul (parents, grandparents, etc.) and furu' (sons, daughters, grandchildren, etc.). Explanation: A person who is an ancestor like father, grandfather, great-grandfather, mother, grandmother, great-grandmother, maternal grandfather, maternal grandmother; or a descendant like son, daughter, grandson, granddaughter, great-grandson, great-granddaughter, grandson-in-law, granddaughter-in-law, great-grandson-in-law, or great-granddaughter-in-law. Since their livelihood is the responsibility of the giver, giving to them is like spending zakat on oneself, therefore giving zakat to these people is not permissible. Proof (1): There is such a deep connection with these people that their maintenance is one's own responsibility. So giving to them is like keeping the zakat wealth with oneself. Therefore, giving zakat wealth to them does not fulfill the zakat obligation. (2) From Ali bin Abi Talib: 'There is no right for a child or a parent in obligatory sadaqah, and whoever has a child or a parent and does not support them, he is disowned.' (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, vol.7 p.45 no.13229). This athar states that furu' (offspring) and usul (parents) have no right in zakat. (3) There is an athar that if one supports those who are usul or furu', giving them zakat does not fulfill the zakat. From Ibn Abbas: 'There is no harm in placing your zakat among your relatives as long as they are not dependents.' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, vol.2 p.412 no.10531; Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq, vol.4 p.88 no.7193). From this athar it is learned that close relatives who are naturally supported cannot be given zakat such that the zakat obligation is fulfilled. Translation: (1197) And his wife. Explanation: The wife is related to the husband and her maintenance is obligatory upon the husband, so giving zakat to the wife is not permissible. Proof (1): From Ibn Abbas: 'There is no harm in placing your zakat among your relatives as long as they are not dependents.' This athar says that one cannot give zakat to those who are dependents. And the wife is dependent on the husband, so the zakat will return to him. Translation: (1198) And his male slave, his mukatib, and such a slave who is partially free. Explanation: Giving zakat to one's own slave is like keeping the zakat with oneself, so one cannot give to one's own slave. One can give zakat to another's mukatib so that he can pay the kitabah and gain freedom. But one cannot give to one's own mukatib slave, because that zakat money will return to the owner, so the giver's zakat has come back to him. Therefore, making the mukatib completely owner of the zakat... (text incomplete)
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage from Thamaratun Najah covers three categories of people to whom the zakat giver cannot pay zakat: (1) One's own ascendants (parents, grandparents, etc.) and descendants (children, grandchildren, etc.), (2) One's spouse, specifically the wife, and (3) One's own slaves, including mukatib slaves (those under a contract of emancipation) and part-free slaves. The ruling is based on the principle that if the recipient is someone whose maintenance is obligatory upon the giver, then giving zakat to them is equivalent to using the zakat for oneself, as the giver would have been obligated to spend on them anyway. Thus the zakat obligation is not fulfilled. The author provides multiple proofs: an athar from Ali (may Allah be pleased with him) stating that parents and children have no right in obligatory sadaqah, and an athar from Ibn Abbas that zakat can be given to relatives except those who are dependents. For the wife, since her nafaqah is the husband's responsibility, the same applies. For slaves, giving zakat to one's own slave simply transfers wealth to oneself indirectly; hence it is invalid. The author distinguishes between one's own slave and another's mukatib: giving to another's mukatib is permissible because it helps the slave without returning to the giver, but giving to one's own mukatib is invalid as the money eventually benefits the owner. The passage ends mid-sentence but the ruling is clear.

Key Terms
اصل (aṣl): Ascendants: parents, grandparents, etc., to whom the giver owes maintenance.
فرع (farʿ): Descendants: children, grandchildren, etc., to whom the giver owes maintenance.
مکاتب (mukātib): A slave who has entered a contract of emancipation (kitabah) with the owner to pay for his freedom.
معتق بعضہ (muʿtaq baʿḍuhu): A slave who has been partially freed (e.g., half-free).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Can a person give zakat to his own wife?
A) Yes, if she is poor
B) Yes, with her consent
C) No, because her nafaqah is his responsibility
D) No, because she is not a relative

Key Takeaway
Zakat cannot be given to anyone whose maintenance you are obligated to provide, as it would effectively return the wealth to yourself.

Zakat — Almsgiving
intermediate
6 min
Zakat Cannot Be Given to One's Usul and Furu'
Explaining the prohibition of giving zakat to one's own ascendants and descendants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on giving zakat to one's own ascendants and descendants and explain the proof from the Companions' statements.

Core Idea
Giving zakat to one's own dependents is like giving it to oneself—the obligation is not discharged.

This passage addresses a critical rule in zakat distribution: you cannot give your zakat to your own ascendants (parents, grandparents) or descendants (children, grandchildren) for whom you are legally responsible for maintenance. The reasoning is that the zakat money would effectively return to you. The passage provides proofs from the statements of 'Ali and Ibn 'Abbas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its scope
Read the passage to determine what the ruling is about. It concerns giving zakat to one's usul (ascendants) and furu' (descendants). Note that it includes all levels: parents, grandparents, children, grandchildren, etc.
The passage uses terms 'asl' and 'far' in Arabic.
2
Understand the legal reasoning (ta'lil)
The text states that because the maintenance (nafaqah) of these relatives is obligatory upon the giver, giving them zakat is like spending the zakat on oneself. Therefore, the zakat obligation is not fulfilled.
This reasoning is unique to the Hanafi school.
3
Examine the first proof (athar from 'Ali)
The passage quotes 'Ali ibn Abi Talib: 'There is no right for a child or a parent in the obligatory charity (zakat).' This indicates that they cannot be recipients of zakat.
4
Examine the second proof (athar from Ibn 'Abbas)
Ibn 'Abbas is quoted: 'There is no harm in placing your zakat among your relatives as long as they are not under your support (dependents).' This implies that if they are dependents, it is not allowed.
This athar establishes the condition of dependency as the decisive factor.
5
Note the practical implication
The conclusion is that giving zakat to such relatives does not discharge the zakat obligation. The zakat must be given to other eligible recipients.
Cross-madhhab: Shafi'is allow giving to poor relatives even if they are dependents.

Source Text

باب المصرف (١١٩٦) واصل المزكي وفرعه
ترجمه : (۱۱۹۶) اور زکوۃ دینے والے کے اصول ( ماں باپ، دادا دادی وغیرہ) اور فروع ( بیٹا بیٹی، پوتا پوتی وغیرہ) تشریح : جو آدمی اصل ہو جیسے باپ، دادا، پر دادا، ماں، دادی، پردادی، نانا، نانی ۔ یا جو آدمی کا فروع ہو، جیسے بیٹا، بیٹی ، پوتا، پوتی ، پر پوتا، پر پوتی، نواسہ، نواسی، پرنواسه، یا پر نواسی ، تو چونکہ ان لوگوں کا نان نفقہ آدمی کے ذمے ہوتا ہے تو ان کو دینا گویا کہ زکوۃ اپنے ہی کے اوپر خرچ کرنا ہوا، اس لئے ان لوگوں کو زکوۃ دینا جائز نہیں ہے۔ وجه (۱) ان لوگوں کے ساتھ اتنا گہرا رابطہ ہوتا ہے کہ ان کا نان و نفقہ بھی اپنے ہی ذمہ ہوتا ہے۔ اس لئے ان لوگوں کو دینا گویا کہ زکوۃ کا مال اپنے ہی پاس رکھ لینا ہے۔ اس لئے زکوۃ کا مال ان لوگوں کو دینے سے زکوۃ کی ادائیگی نہیں ہوگی (۲) عـن عــلــی بـن طالب : ليس لولد و لا والد حق في صدقة مفروضة ، و من كان له ولد أو والد فلم يصله فهو عاق (سنن بیہقی ، باب لا يعطيها من تلزمه نفقته من ولده ووالديه من تهم الفقراء والمساكين، ج سابع ص ۴۵ نمبر ۱۳۲۲۹) اس اثر میں ہے کہ فروع یعنی اولاد، اور اصول یعنی والد وغیرہ کا حق زکوۃ میں نہیں ہے۔ (۳) اثر میں ہے کہ جن لوگوں کی کفالت کرتا ہو اور اصول و فروع میں سے ہوں ان کو زکوۃ دینے سے زکوۃ کی ادائیگی نہیں ہوگی ۔ عن ابن عباس قال لا بأس ان تجعل زکوتک فی ذوی قرابتک مالم يكونوا في عيالك (مصنف ابن ابي هيئة ۹۶ ما قالوافی الرجل یدفع زکوتہ الی قرابته ج ثانی ص ۴۱۲، نمبر ۱۰۵۳۱ر مصنف عبدالرزاق ، باب لمن الزكوة ج رابع ص ۸۸ نمبر ۷۱۹۳ ) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ جو قریب کے رشتہ دار ہوں اور اس کی قدرتی طور پر کفالت بھی کرتا ہو تو اس کو زکوۃ دینے سے زکوۃ کی ادائیگی نہیں ہوگی۔
Chapter of the Disbursement (1196) And the giver's ascendant and descendant. Translation: (1196) And the giver's ascendants (mother, father, grandfather, grandmother, etc.) and descendants (son, daughter, grandson, granddaughter, etc.). Explanation: A person who is an ascendant, such as father, grandfather, great-grandfather, mother, grandmother, great-grandmother, maternal grandfather, maternal grandmother; or a person who is a descendant, such as son, daughter, grandson, granddaughter, great-grandson, great-granddaughter, grandson through daughter, granddaughter through daughter, etc. Since the sustenance and maintenance of these people is the responsibility of the person, giving it to them is like spending the zakat on oneself. Therefore, it is not permissible to give zakat to these people. Reasons: (1) There is such a close relationship with these people that their maintenance is also one's own responsibility. So giving to them is like keeping the zakat money with oneself. Hence, giving zakat to these people does not fulfill the zakat. (2) From 'Ali ibn Abi Talib: 'There is no right for a child or a parent in the obligatory charity (zakat), and whoever has a child or parent and does not support them, he is undutiful.' (Sunan al-Bayhaqi) This athar indicates that descendants (children) and ascendants (parents, etc.) have no right in zakat. (3) Another athar indicates that giving zakat to those whose maintenance one undertakes among ascendants and descendants does not fulfill the zakat. From Ibn 'Abbas: 'There is no harm in placing your zakat among your relatives as long as they are not under your support (i.e., dependents).' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, Musannaf 'Abd al-Razzaq) This athar shows that if the relatives are naturally under one's support, then giving zakat to them does not fulfill the zakat.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof explaining that zakat cannot be given to one's own ascendants (usul) and descendants (furu'). The text first defines usul as parents, grandparents, etc., and furu' as children, grandchildren, etc. The reasoning is that since the maintenance (nafaqah) of these individuals is obligatory upon the zakat giver, giving zakat to them is effectively returning the zakat to oneself. Therefore, the zakat is not considered discharged. The text supports this with two athar (narrations from Companions): one from 'Ali ibn Abi Talib stating that neither child nor parent has a right to obligatory charity, and one from Ibn 'Abbas permitting giving zakat to relatives only if they are not dependents (i.e., not under one's support). This establishes the Hanafi ruling that zakat to one's own usul and furu' is invalid.

Key Terms
اصل (asl): In this context, ascendants of the zakat giver: parents, grandparents, great-grandparents, maternal grandparents, etc.
فرع (far'): Descendants of the zakat giver: children, grandchildren, great-grandchildren, etc., including through daughters.
مصرف (masrif): The category of recipients to whom zakat may be disbursed. This chapter is about the permissible and impermissible recipients.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is it impermissible to give zakat to one's own son?
A) Because the son is already rich
B) Because the son's maintenance is obligatory on the father, so the zakat effectively returns to the father
C) Because the son is a close relative
D) Because the son is not deserving of zakat

Key Takeaway
Zakat cannot be given to one's own ascendants or descendants whose maintenance is obligatory upon the giver, as it is like keeping the money for oneself.

Zakat — Almsgiving

Zakat Cannot Be Given to One's Wife
Explaining that zakat cannot be given to one's wife

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on giving zakat to one's wife and explain the legal reasoning (dalil) behind it.

Core Idea
Giving zakat to one's wife is invalid because her maintenance is obligatory on the husband—the zakat effectively returns to him.

The wife is a close relative of the husband, and her nafaqah (maintenance) is a legal obligation upon him. This creates a unique situation where giving her zakat would not truly transfer ownership, as the financial benefit circulates back to the husband. The Hanafi school, relying on an athar from Ibn Abbas, therefore rules that such a payment does not fulfill the zakat obligation. In this lesson, we will examine the evidence and understand why this ruling stands.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and its Ruling
First, identify the act under discussion: a husband giving his zakat to his wife. State the Hanafi ruling: it is batil (invalid) and does not discharge the zakat obligation.
The text explicitly says 'jā'iz nahīṁ hai' (not permissible).
2
Locate the Dalil
The author cites an athar (tradition) from Ibn Abbas: 'There is no harm in placing your zakat among your relatives as long as they are not under your support (dependents).' This is transmitted in Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah and Musannaf 'Abd al-Razzaq. Identify it as a qawl al-sahabi (saying of a Companion), which is a valid source in Hanafi usul.
The precise wording: 'مالم يكونوا في عيالك'
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
The operative phrase in the dalil is 'مالم يكونوا في عيالك' (as long as they are not under your support). The wife is unquestionably under the husband's support (kafalat) because her nafaqah is wajib on him. Therefore, she falls into the category of those who cannot receive zakat. The reasoning is that the zakat would effectively return to the giver.
The text explicitly states: 'اور بیوی شوہر کی کفالت میں ہے اس لئے زکوۃ اس پر ہی لوٹ آئے گی۔'
4
Note the Ikhtilaf
Some other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) allow giving zakat to one's wife if she is poor and not receiving maintenance. The Hanafi school rejects this because the obligation of nafaqah exists regardless of actual payment.
The disagreement stems from whether the wife is considered a 'dependent' in the technical sense required for the prohibition.
5
Evaluate the Tarjih
The Hanafi position is the mufta bihi (relied-upon) within the school. It is based on the clear wording of the athar and the legal principle that zakat cannot be used to fulfill an already existing obligation of the giver.
The tarjih is supported by the authority of the early Hanafi masters and consistent application in the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱۱۹۷) وزوجته
ترجمه : (۱۱۹۷) اور اس کی بیوی کو ۔
تشریح : بیوی شوہر کے قرابت میں ہے اور اس کا نفقہ شوہر پر لازم ہے اس لئے بیوی کو بھی زکوۃ دینا جائز نہیں ہے۔ وجه (۱) عن ابن عباس قال لا بأس ان تجعل زكوتک فی ذوی قرابتک مالم يكونوا في عيالك (مصنف ابن ابی شیبه ۹۶ ما قالوا فی الرجل یدفع زکوتہ الی قرابته ج ثانی ص ۴۱۲، نمبر ۱۰۵۳۱ر مصنف عبد الرزاق ، باب لمن الزكوة ج رابع ص ۸۸ نمبر ۷۱۹۳ ) اس اثر میں ہے کہ جو کفالت میں ہو ان کو زکوۃ نہیں دے سکتے۔ اور بیوی شوہر کی کفالت میں ہے اس لئے زکوۃ اس پر ہی لوٹ آئے گی۔
(1197) And his wife. Translation: (1197) And his wife. Explanation: The wife is related to the husband and her maintenance is obligatory upon the husband, therefore giving zakat to the wife is also not permissible. Reason: (1) From Ibn 'Abbas: 'There is no harm in placing your zakat among your relatives as long as they are not under your support (dependents).' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, Musannaf 'Abd al-Razzaq) This athar indicates that those who are under one's support cannot be given zakat. And the wife is under the husband's support, so the zakat will return to him.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof explaining that zakat cannot be given to one's wife. The reasoning is identical to that of usul and furu': since the wife's maintenance (nafaqah) is obligatory upon the husband, giving her zakat would mean the money effectively returns to him. The text cites the same athar from Ibn 'Abbas mentioned previously, which states giving zakat to dependents is not valid. Therefore, the Hanafi ruling is that zakat to one's own wife is invalid.

Key Terms
زوجة (zawjah): Wife of the zakat giver. In this context, she is considered a dependent whose nafaqah is obligatory on the husband.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is it not permissible for a husband to give zakat to his wife according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because the wife is not considered poor
B) Because the wife's maintenance is the husband's responsibility, so the zakat returns to him
C) Because the wife is not a relative
D) Because the wife owns property herself

Key Takeaway
Giving zakat to one's wife is invalid because her maintenance is obligatory on the husband—the zakat effectively returns to him.

Zakat Cannot Be Given to One's Own Slaves
Ruling with proof on zakat recipients

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on giving zakat to one's own slaves and explain the underlying principle of full ownership using the cited athar.

Core Idea
Zakat requires making a poor Muslim full owner; giving to your own slave fails because property returns to you.

This lesson explains a clear Hanafi ruling: you cannot give zakat to your own slaves, whether fully owned (mamluk), contracted for freedom (mukatab), or partially freed (mu'athtaq ba'd). The core reason is that zakat must transfer full ownership to a needy recipient, and a slave's property belongs to the master. The text supports this with athar (reports from early authorities) and a key fiqh principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is giving zakat to one's own slave (mamluk, mukatab, mu'athtaq ba'd, mudabbar, umm walad). The ruling is that it is invalid (batil) and does not fulfill the zakat obligation.
The text explicitly says 'jā'iz nahīñ' (not permissible).
2
Locate the dalil
Two athar are cited: (1) a long report from al-Thawri listing forbidden uses of zakat, including giving to one's own mukatab; (2) a short report from al-Hasan: 'Do not give a slave or a mushrik from zakat.' These are found in Musannaf 'Abd al-Razzaq.
These are statements of Tabi'een, not Prophetic hadith, but they are accepted as evidence by the Hanafi school.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The athar from al-Thawri explicitly includes 'nor give it to your mukatab.' The athar from al-Hasan prohibits giving zakat to a slave (presumably one's own). The underlying reasoning is that the slave cannot own property independently; the zakat effectively returns to the giver. The text states: 'Giving to one's own slave is like keeping the zakat with oneself.'
The principle is that zakat requires tamlik (making owner), which is impossible with one's own slave.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may allow giving zakat to one's own slave if the slave is poor and the master is not obligated to support him. The Hanafi school takes a stricter view because the slave's acquisitions belong to the master.
The cross-madhhab status is mukhtalaf fih (disputed).
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi (operative for fatwa) and is based on the general principle that zakat must transfer full ownership. The athar provide explicit support. No intra-Hanafi dispute is recorded.
Authority weight: zahir al-riwayah (from the core Hanafi texts).

Source Text

(مصنف عبدالرزاق ، باب لمن الزكاة ، ج رابع ص ۸۹ نمبر ۷۱۹۷ ) اس اثر میں ہے کہ اپنے غلام کو زکوۃ نہ دے۔ کیونکہ وہ مال خود اسی کی طرف واپس آجائے گا۔ اصول: زکوۃ کی ادائیگی کے لئے مسلمان غریب کو مالک بنانا ضروری ہے۔
(1198) And his slave, and his mukatab, and one partially freed. Translation: (1198) And his owned slave, and mukatab, and such a slave some part of whom is freed, giving zakat to them is also not permissible. Explanation: Giving to one's own slave is like keeping the zakat with oneself, therefore one cannot give to his own slave. One can give zakat to another person's mukatab so that he may pay the kitabah money and gain freedom, but one cannot give to his own mukatab slave because that zakat amount would come back to the owner, so the zakat of the giver returns to him. Thus, making the mukatab fully own the zakat is not achieved, so one cannot give to his own mukatab. Similarly, one cannot give zakat to his own mudabbar slave because that slave's money will then become the owner's. Likewise, one cannot give zakat to his own umm walad slave-girl because all of that slave-girl's property belongs to the master. So their zakat returns to them, and full ownership of the zakat is not achieved, therefore one cannot give zakat to these people. Reason: (1) For all of this, there is the statement of a Tabi'i: From al-Thawri: 'A man should not give the zakat of his wealth to those for whom he is compelled to spend on relatives, nor should he give it for a deceased's shroud, nor for a deceased's debt, nor for building a mosque, nor for buying a Quran, nor should he perform Hajj with it, nor should he give it to his mukatab, nor should he purchase with it a slave to free, nor should he give it to Jews or Christians, nor should he hire with it a carrier to carry it from one place to another.' (Musannaf 'Abd al-Razzaq) This hadith states: [1] Do not give your zakat to those whose maintenance is your responsibility; [2] Not for a deceased's shroud; [3] Not for a deceased's debt; [4] Not for building a mosque; [5] Not for buying the Quran; [7] Do not give to your mukatab (one can give to another's mukatab); [8] Do not purchase a slave to free with it; [9] Do not give to Jews or Christians, i.e., to disbelievers; [10] Do not hire an animal for yourself with it. (2) From al-Hasan: 'Do not give a slave or a mushrik from zakat.' (Musannaf 'Abd al-Razzaq) This athar indicates that one should not give zakat to his own slave, because that wealth will return to him. Principle: For the fulfillment of zakat, making a poor Muslim the owner (of the zakat) is necessary.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof explaining that zakat cannot be given to one's own slaves, including a full slave (mamluk), a mukatab (slave who has contracted to purchase his freedom), and a mu'athtaq ba'd (a slave partially freed). The reasoning is that giving zakat to one's own slave is like keeping the money, as the slave's property ultimately belongs to the master. The text distinguishes between one's own mukatab (invalid) and another person's mukatab (valid) because in the latter case the slave fully owns the zakat. The same applies to mudabbar (slave promised freedom upon master's death) and umm walad (slave-girl mother of master's child). The text then provides a longer athar from al-Thawri listing several impermissible uses of zakat, including giving to one's own mukatab and purchasing a slave to free. A second athar from al-Hasan explicitly prohibits giving zakat to one's own slave. The underlying principle is that zakat requires making a poor Muslim the full owner of the wealth, which is not achieved in these cases.

Key Terms
مملوك (mamluk): A full slave owned by the zakat giver. The slave's earnings and property belong to the master.
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has contracted with his master to pay a sum for his freedom. If the mukatab is one's own, giving him zakat is invalid because the money may revert to the master; if he belongs to another, it is valid.
معتق بعضه (mu'athtaq ba'duh): A slave who is partially freed, e.g., half-free, half-slave. Giving zakat to such a slave is invalid for the same reason.
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave who has been promised freedom upon the master's death. His property belongs to the master during the master's lifetime.
ام ولد (umm walad): A slave-girl who has borne her master's child. Her property belongs to the master.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, giving zakat to which of the following is invalid?
A) A mukatab slave belonging to another person
B) One's own mudabbar slave
C) A poor Muslim relative whom you are not required to support
D) A free poor Muslim neighbor

Key Takeaway
Zakat must be given to a recipient who can fully own the wealth; giving to one's own slave fails because the slave's property belongs to the master.

Zakat Cannot Be Used for Deceased's Shroud or Debt
Ruling with proof on zakat for deceased's shroud and debt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on using zakat for a deceased person's shroud or debt and distinguish between direct (invalid) and indirect (valid) methods.

Core Idea
Zakat must be given to a living owner; a deceased cannot own, so direct payment for shroud or debt is invalid.

This lesson addresses a common question: Can zakat be used to cover the funeral expenses or debts of a deceased person? The Hanafi school has a clear ruling based on the fundamental requirement that zakat must reach a living owner. Let's explore the source text to understand why direct payment is invalid and how one may lawfully assist via a living recipient.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What is being ruled upon? Directly using zakat for a deceased's shroud or debt. The ruling is that this is invalid (batil).
Note the contrast with the indirect method which is valid.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites the statement of al-Thawri as proof. Although not a primary text, it is a recognized legal opinion in the Hanafi school.
The condition of tamlik (making the recipient owner) is the underlying principle.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does the condition of tamlik prove the prohibition? A deceased person is legally incapable of owning property; therefore, giving zakat directly for his shroud or debt does not fulfill the requirement of tamlik.
The indirect method works because the poor person becomes the owner first.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱۱۹۹) وكفن ميت وقضاء دينه
ترجمه : (۱۱۹۹) اور میت کے کفن اور اس کے قرض کی ادائیگی ۔
تشریح : کسی غریب کوزکوۃ دیکر اس کو کہیں کہ میت کا کفن دو یا قرض ادا کرو تو جائز ہے، لیکن خود کے کفن میں دینا یا اس کا قرض ادا کرنا جائز نہیں ہے، کیونکہ زکوۃ کے مال کا مالک بنانا ضروری ہے، اور یہاں میت مر چکا ہے اس لئے اس کو مالک نہیں بنا سکتے اس لئے اس کو زکوۃ دینے سے زکوۃ کی ادائیگی نہیں ہوگی ۔ اس کے لئے قول ثوری او پر گزرا ۔
(1199) And a deceased's shroud and repayment of his debt. Translation: (1199) And the deceased's shroud and repayment of his debt. Explanation: It is permissible to give zakat to a poor person and then tell him to give the deceased's shroud or pay the debt, but it is not permissible to give zakat directly for the shroud or to pay his debt, because it is necessary to make someone the owner of the zakat wealth, and here the deceased has died, so he cannot be made the owner. Therefore, giving zakat to him (the deceased) does not fulfill the zakat. The statement of al-Thawri has already passed regarding this.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof explaining that zakat cannot be used directly for a deceased person's shroud (kafan) or to repay the deceased's debt (dayn). The key principle is that zakat must be given to a living person who becomes its owner. A deceased person cannot own property, so spending zakat on their shroud or debt directly is invalid. However, the text notes that if one gives the zakat money to a poor living person and then instructs him to use it for the deceased's shroud or debt, that is permissible because the poor person becomes the owner first. The proof cited is the earlier statement of al-Thawri, which explicitly prohibits giving zakat for a deceased's shroud or debt.

Key Terms
كفن ميت (kafan mayyit): The shroud used to bury a deceased Muslim. Zakat money cannot be spent directly on it because the deceased cannot own the money.
قضاء دين (qada' dayn): Repayment of a debt owed by the deceased. Zakat cannot be used directly for this purpose for the same reason.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Zakat Cannot Be Used to Purchase a Slave to Free
Explaining the invalidity of using zakat for slave purchase

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on using zakat to buy a slave to free and explain why it does not fulfill the zakat obligation.

Core Idea
Zakat requires giving ownership to a poor person; buying a slave to free does not achieve that.

Many assume that any charitable act, such as freeing a slave, can fulfill the obligation of zakat. However, the Hanafi school rigorously requires that zakat be placed in the ownership (tamlik) of a poor recipient. In this lesson, we explore why using zakat to purchase a slave for emancipation falls short, supported by the practice of early authorities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine the specific action being evaluated: using zakat money to purchase a slave with the intention of freeing him. According to the Hanafi school, this does not discharge the zakat obligation.
The ruling is that the zakat is not fulfilled (batil in the sense of not meeting the obligation).
2
Locate the dalil
The text cites two athar: the statement of al-Thawri and the disapproval of Ibrahim an-Nakha'i. These serve as proof for the Hanafi position.
The athar from al-Thawri explicitly forbids purchasing a slave to free with zakat.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The operative principle is that zakat must involve tamlik (making the poor person the owner). Buying a slave does not make the slave the owner of the money; instead, the money is used to buy an asset, and the slave becomes free but never possessed the zakat funds.
The condition of tamlik is fundamental in Hanafi zakat rules.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some other schools may allow this under the category of 'fi al-riqab' (freeing slaves) in zakat. The Hanafi school limits that category to giving zakat directly to a mukatab slave (who is purchasing his freedom).
The practical difference is significant – the Hanafi ruling restricts the 'slaves' category to mukatab only.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position is based on the clear condition of tamlik and the authoritative athar. It is the mufta bihi (operative opinion) in the school.
The ruling is from the zahir al-riwayah, making it the standard position.

Reason: (1) From al-Thawri: 'A man should not give the zakat of his wealth to those for whom he is compelled to spend on relatives... nor should he purchase with it a slave to free.' (2) In another athar: From Ibrahim an-Nakha'i: 'He used to dislike that one should buy a slave to free from his zakat money.' (Musannaf)

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof explaining that zakat cannot be used to purchase a slave with the intention of freeing him (i'taq). The reason is that zakat requires giving wealth to a poor person who becomes its owner. In this case, the slave becomes free but was never made the owner of the zakat; the money was used to purchase the slave, not given to a recipient. The text cites the athar of al-Thawri, which includes 'nor should he purchase with it a slave to free,' and also the practice of Ibrahim an-Nakha'i who disapproved of this. Therefore, the Hanafi ruling is that such an action does not discharge the zakat obligation.

Key Terms
قنّ (qinn): A slave who is owned outright. In this context, a slave that one intends to purchase with zakat money and then free.
يُعتق (yu'taq): Is freed. The act of freeing a slave after purchase.

Zakat Not Permitted for Shroud or Debt of the Deceased
Ruling with proof that zakat cannot be given for deceased's shroud or debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on giving zakat for a deceased's shroud or debt, and explain why the condition of tamlik (ownership transfer) is essential.

Core Idea
Zakat requires making a living eligible person owner; a deceased cannot own, so direct payment for shroud or debt is invalid.

This passage from Thamaratun Najah addresses a common practical question: can one use zakat funds to directly pay for a deceased person's funeral shroud or settle their debts? The text clarifies the Hanafi position, rooted in the condition of tamlik (ownership transfer). By the end, you will understand the ruling and the underlying principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? The act of giving zakat directly for a deceased's shroud or debt. The ruling is that it does not fulfill the zakat obligation (invalid).
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the statement of Sufyan al-Thawri (a tabi'i), which explicitly prohibits giving zakat for the shroud or debt of a deceased.
The exact wording is: 'الرجل لا يعطى زكوة ماله ... ولا يعطيها في كفن ميت ولا دين ميت'
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does this dalil prove the ruling? The prohibition implies that making someone owner (tamlik) is required; the deceased cannot be made owner, so the prohibition is understood as a consequence of missing tamlik.
The Hanafi school derives that tamlik is a condition for zakat validity.
4
Distinguish the valid alternative
The text mentions that giving zakat to a poor person and instructing him to use it for the deceased's shroud or debt is valid. This is because the poor person becomes the owner of the zakat.
The key is that the living poor person receives ownership, not the deceased.
5
Note the cross-madhhab aspect
Other schools may have different views; some may allow direct payment. The Hanafi school insists on tamlik as essential.
This ruling is consistent with the standard Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱۱۹۹) وكفن ميت وقضاء دينه 
ترجمه : (۱۱۹۹) اور میت کے کفن اور اس کے قرض کی ادائیگی ۔ 
تشریح : کسی غریب کوزکوۃ دیکر اس کو کہیں کہ میت کا کفن دو یا قرض ادا کرو تو جائز ہے، لیکن خود کے کفن میں دینا یا اس کا قرض ادا کرنا جائز نہیں ہے، کیونکہ زکوۃ کے مال کا مالک بنانا ضروری ہے، اور یہاں میت مر چکا ہے اس لئے اس کو مالک نہیں بنا سکتے اس لئے اس کو زکوۃ دینے سے زکوۃ کی ادائیگی نہیں ہوگی ۔ اس کے لئے قول ثوری او پر گزرا ۔

(1199) And the shroud of a deceased and payment of his debt. Translation: (1199) And the shroud of a deceased and payment of his debt. Explanation: It is permissible to give zakat to a poor person and tell him to give the shroud of the deceased or pay his debt, but it is not permissible to give it directly for the shroud of the deceased or to pay his debt directly, because it is necessary to make someone the owner of the zakat wealth, and here the deceased has died so he cannot be made the owner, thus giving zakat to him does not fulfill the zakat. For this, the statement of Thawri has passed above. Proof: (1) From Thawri, he said: A man should not give zakat of his wealth ... and he should not give it for the shroud of a deceased nor for the debt of a deceased (Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq, Bab li-man al-Zakah, vol. 4, p. 89, no. 7200).
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage from Thamaratun Najah explains the ruling that zakat cannot be used to directly pay for a deceased person's shroud or debts. The key principle is that zakat must be given to a living eligible recipient, making them the actual owner of the wealth. Since the deceased is dead, he cannot own property. The text clarifies that if you give zakat to a poor person and instruct him to use it for the deceased's shroud or debt, it is valid because the poor person becomes the owner and then uses it as instructed. However, direct payment for the deceased (e.g., buying the shroud and giving it to the funeral) does not fulfill zakat. The proof is the statement of Sufyan al-Thawri, who explicitly prohibits giving zakat for the shroud or debt of a deceased. This is further supported by the general principle that zakat must be given to the living eligible.

Key Terms
كفن ميت (kafan mayyit): The shroud used to wrap the deceased for burial.
تمليك (tamlik): Transferring ownership of the zakat wealth to the recipient.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, does giving zakat directly to pay for the shroud of a deceased person fulfill the zakat obligation?
A) Yes, because it is a necessary expense.
B) No, because the deceased cannot be made the owner of the zakat.
C) Yes, if the deceased was poor.
D) No, but it is considered a good deed.

Key Takeaway
Zakat must be given to a living eligible owner; direct payment for a deceased's shroud or debt does not fulfill the obligation.

Zakat Used to Buy a Slave for Emancipation Is Invalid
States ruling with proof that buying a slave with zakat to free him is invalid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on using zakat to purchase a slave for emancipation and explain why it is invalid based on the condition of tamlik.

Core Idea
Zakat must involve tamlik; buying a slave to free bypasses this and invalidates the zakat.

Zakat is not merely a charitable act; it is a legal obligation that requires the transfer of ownership (tamlik) to a poor person. The noble act of freeing a slave, though meritorious, cannot substitute for this condition. This lesson examines the Hanafi position that using zakat funds to purchase a slave for emancipation fails to fulfill the zakat obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Determine what action the passage rules upon: using zakat money to buy a slave and then freeing him. State the ruling: the zakat is not fulfilled (batil).
2
Locate the dalil
The passage cites two proofs: the statement of Sufyan al-Thawri prohibiting buying a 'nasamah' (living being) to free, and the dislike of Ibrahim al-Nakha'i of buying a slave with zakat to free.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Understand how these statements prove the ruling. They indicate that the purpose of zakat is tamlik, not freeing slaves directly. The prohibition and dislike reinforce that no poor person becomes owner.
4
Apply the condition of tamlik
The passage explicitly states that the zakat is invalid 'because there is no making a poor person owner (tamlik).' This is the core legal reason (illah).
Tamlik is a fundamental condition in Hanafi zakat law.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
While the Hanafi school holds this position, other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may allow using zakat for 'fir riqab' (freeing slaves) as part of the eight categories of zakat recipients.
The source does not detail the opposing view; this step is added for context.

Source Text

تشریح : زکوۃ سے غلام خریدے اور اس کو آزاد کرے اس طرح زکوۃ ادا نہیں ہوگی ، کیونکہ اس میں کسی غریب کو مالک بنانا نہیں ہوا

Proof: (1) From Thawri, he said: A man should not give zakat of his wealth ... and do not buy with it a living being to free. (Musannaf Abd al-Razzaq, Bab min al-Zakah, vol. 4, p. 89, no. 7200) (2) From Ibrahim: He disliked that one should buy with his zakat a slave to free. (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, no. 10543)
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage explains that zakat cannot be used to purchase a slave for the purpose of emancipation. The reason is that zakat must be given to a poor person, making them the owner. In this case, the slave is purchased and then freed, so no poor person becomes the owner of the zakat wealth. The text cites the statement of Sufyan al-Thawri prohibiting buying a 'nasamah' (a living being) to free, and the dislike of Ibrahim al-Nakha'i for buying a slave with zakat to free. These proofs reinforce the Hanafi position that tamlik is essential.

Key Terms
قنّ (qinn): A male slave.
نسمة (nasamah): A living being, specifically a slave in this context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) No, because the zakat must be given directly to a poor person.
C) Yes, if the slave is poor.
D) No, but only for a mukatab.

Key Takeaway
Zakat requires tamlik to a poor person; buying a slave to free him does not achieve this, so the zakat obligation remains unfulfilled.

Giving Zakat with Ijtihad: When Effort Excuses Error
States ruling that zakat suffices if given with ijtihad even if recipient is ineligible, except for own slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that zakat given with ijtihad is valid even if the recipient turns out ineligible, and identify the exception for one's own slave or mukatab.

Core Idea
When you give zakat after genuine effort, an honest mistake does not invalidate it—except if the recipient is your own property.

Zakat must be given to eligible recipients, but what happens if you later discover you were mistaken? The Hanafi school provides a merciful rule: if you made a genuine effort (taharri) to find an eligible person, your zakat is valid even if the recipient was not actually poor. However, there is a critical exception: if the recipient turns out to be your own slave or mukatab, the zakat is not valid, because the benefit ultimately returns to you.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is giving zakat to someone believed to be eligible. The ruling is that it suffices (اجزأه) if the giver performed ijtihad (taharri).
2
Recognize the condition of taharri
Taharri means making a reasonable effort to ascertain eligibility. Without it, the ruling may differ. The text implies that effort is required for the exception to apply.
3
Note the exception and its rationale
The exception is when the recipient is the giver's own slave or mukatab. Because the slave's property belongs to the master, the zakat would effectively benefit the giver himself, defeating the purpose of zakat.
4
Examine the dalil from hadith
The hadith of Ma'an ibn Yazid shows that the Prophet (peace be upon him) accepted the father's intention when his charity accidentally went to his son. This supports that intention and effort are key.
5
Understand the cross-madhhab perspective
Other schools may require re-giving if the recipient is found ineligible, but the Hanafi school relies on the principle that intention and due diligence override the error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱۲۰۱) ولو دفع بتحرٍ لمن ظنّه مصرفا فظهر بخلافه اجزأه (۱۲۰۲ ) الا ان يكون عبده ومكاتبه . 
ترجمه : (۱۲۰۱) ۔ اور اگر زکوۃ ایسے آدمی کو دی جس کے متعلق یہ گمان ہے کہ وہ مستحق ہے پھر اس کے خلاف ظاہر ہوا تو زکوۃ ادا ہو جائے گی۔ 
ترجمه : (۱۲۰۲)، الا یہ کہ وہ اس کا غلام ہو یا مکاتب ہو ( تو ادا نہ ہو گی )۔ 
تشریح : کسی نے فقیر گمان کرتے ہوئے زکوۃ دیا کہ یہ مستحق ہے لیکن بعد میں معلوم ہوا کہ یہ مستحق نہیں ہے پھر بھی اگر تحقیق کے بعد دیا تھا اور بعد میں خطا ظاہر ہوگئی تو زکوۃ کی ادائیگی ہو جائے گی۔ حنفیہ کے نزدیک دوبارہ دینے کی ضرورت نہیں۔ اور اگر ظاہر ہو گیا کہ یہ اپنا غلام ہے، یا اپنا مکا تب ہے تو زکوۃ ادا نہیں ہوئی۔ 
وجه : (۱) عن معن بن یزید حدثه ... و كان ابي يزيد اخرج دنانير يتصدق بها فوضعها عند رجل في المسجد فجئت فاخذتها فاتيته بها فقال والله ما ایاک اردت فخاصمته الی رسول الله فقال لک مانویت یا یزید ولک ما اخذت یا معن (بخاری شریف، باب اذا تصدق علی ابنہ وصولا یشعر ص ۱۹۱ نمبر ۱۴۲۲) (۲) عن الحسن في الرجل يعطى زكوته الى فقير ثم يتبين له انه غنى قال اجزى عنه. (مصنف ابن ابی شیبۃ ۹۷ ما قالوافی الرجل یعطی زکوی یعنی وھولا يعلم ج خانی ص ۴۱۳ ، نمبر ۱۰۵۴۳)
(1201) And if he gives by ijtihad to someone he thinks is eligible, then it appears contrary, it suffices. (1202) Except if it is his slave or his mukatab. Translation: (1201) And if zakat is given to a person about whom it is thought that he is eligible, then it turns out contrary, the zakat will be fulfilled. (1202) Except if it is his slave or mukatab (then it will not be fulfilled). Explanation: If one gave zakat thinking a person is poor and eligible, but later it turned out he is not eligible, and if he had given after investigation, then when the error appears, the zakat will be fulfilled. According to the Hanafis, there is no need to give again. And if it appears that he is his own slave or mukatab, then the zakat is not fulfilled. Proof: (1) From Ma'an ibn Yazid: ... his father Yazid took out dinars to give in charity and placed them with a man in the mosque, so I (Ma'an) came and took them. He said, 'By Allah, I did not intend you.' So I argued with him before the Messenger of Allah, and he said: 'You have what you intended, O Yazid, and you have what you took, O Ma'an.' (Sahih Bukhari, Bab idha tasaddaqa 'ala ibnihi, p. 191, no. 1422) (2) From Hasan: Regarding a man who gives his zakat to a poor person, then it becomes clear to him that he is rich, he said: It suffices for him. (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, no. 10543)
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage addresses the case where a person gives zakat based on ijtihad (taharri, making an effort to determine eligibility) to someone he believes is a valid recipient, but later it turns out the person was not eligible. According to the Hanafi school, the zakat is still valid if the giver exercised due diligence. This is based on the hadith of Ma'an ibn Yazid, where the father's zakat accidentally went to his son, and the Prophet confirmed the father's intention sufficed. Similarly, a report from Hasan al-Basri states that if one gives zakat thinking the recipient is poor but later finds he is rich, it suffices. However, there is an exception: if the recipient turns out to be the giver's own slave or mukatab, the zakat is not valid because those individuals cannot truly own the wealth (the slave's wealth belongs to the master, and the mukatab's wealth eventually goes to the master). So the giver must repeat the zakat in that case.

Key Terms
تحرٍ (taharri): Making an effort to ascertain the eligibility of a zakat recipient.
مکاتب (mukatab): A slave who has made a contract with his master to purchase his freedom in installments.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person gives zakat to a relative he believes is poor after making an effort to investigate, but later finds out the relative is wealthy. According to the Hanafi school, is the zakat fulfilled?
A) Yes, because he made an effort (taharri).
B) No, because the recipient was not eligible.
C) Yes, if the relative is not his slave.
D) No, because he must investigate more thoroughly.

Key Takeaway
Zakat given with genuine effort (taharri) is valid even if the recipient turns out ineligible, except when the recipient is one's own slave or mukatab.

The Dislike of Enriching a Poor Person Through Zakat
Ruling statement on the dislike of ighna' in zakat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on giving zakat that leaves a poor person with a nisab after meeting basic needs, and identify the conditions that trigger the dislike.

Core Idea
Giving zakat that leaves a surplus beyond nisab is makruh tanzihan – zakat fulfills needs, not creates wealth.

This lesson examines a nuanced ruling in the distribution of zakat: while zakat is obligatory and its recipients are deserving, the amount given should not elevate them to the level of zakat-payers themselves. The Hanafi school considers such enrichment (ighna') as makruh tanzihan, provided certain conditions are met. We will unpack the precise conditions that make this act disliked, and understand the rationale behind the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act
The act under discussion is 'ighna'' – giving zakat to a poor person (faqir) in such a way that after using the zakat, he is no longer poor but becomes wealthy enough to have a nisab (the minimum threshold for zakat obligation).
2
Determine the Ruling
The text states that this act is 'makruh' – disliked. In Hanafi terminology, this refers to karaha tanzihiyya (slightly disliked, closer to permissible but discouraged).
Some other schools may consider it haram or permissible; Hanafi holds it as makruh tanzih.
3
Analyze the Conditions
The dislikedness is conditional. First, the zakat recipient must first pay off any personal debts from the zakat money. Second, he must provide for each of his dependents (family members) an amount less than a nisab. Only after these two deductions, if a nisab remains, then the giver has made him rich and it is makruh.
The dependents' share is less than nisab because the goal is to cover needs, not make them wealthy.
4
Understand the Exception
If after paying debts and providing for dependents no nisab remains, then the zakat is not disliked – it remains permissible (mubah) or even recommended as it meets essential needs.
The ruling hinges on the remaining surplus, not the absolute amount given.
5
Grasp the Wisdom
The rationale is that zakat is prescribed to help the poor meet their immediate needs, not to amass wealth. Making a poor person wealthy contradicts the spirit of zakat which aims at a circulation of wealth and preventing hoarding.
This principle aligns with the broader objective of zakat to close the gap between rich and poor, not to create new wealthy individuals from zakat funds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱۲۰۳) وكره الاغنياء وهو ان يفضل للفقير نصاب بعد قضاء دينه وبعد اعطاء كل فرد من عياله ابن ابی هیئتة 
ترجمه : (۱۲۰۳) فقیر کو مالدار بنادینا مکروہ ہے، اور اس کی صورت یہ ہے کہ فقیر کے پاس اس کے دین ادا ہونے کے بعد اور اس کے عیال میں سے ہر فرد کو نصاب سے کم دینے کے بعد نصاب کی مقدار بچ جائے ۔ ورنہ مکروہ نہیں ہے۔ 
تشریح : جس فقیر کوزکوۃ دی اس نے اپنا قرض ادا کیا، اور اس کی کفالت میں جتنے لوگ تھے سب کو دو دوسو درہم سے کم دینے کے
(1203) And making a poor person rich is disliked, and that is that there remains for the poor person a nisab after paying his debt and after giving each of his dependents [the amount of] Ibn Abi Ha'itah? Translation: (1203) Making a poor person rich is disliked, and its form is that after the poor person pays his debt and after giving each of his dependents less than the nisab, an amount of nisab remains. Otherwise it is not disliked. Explanation: The poor person to whom zakat was given paid his debt, and all those under his care were given less than two or two hundred dirhams.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage discusses the disliked nature (makruh tanzihan) of giving so much zakat to a single poor person that he becomes wealthy, i.e., he has a nisab (minimum threshold for zakat eligibility) remaining after meeting his essential needs. The text specifies that first, the poor person uses the zakat to pay off any debts he owes, and second, he provides for each of his dependents an amount less than the nisab (which is 200 dirhams or equivalent). After that, if he still has a nisab left, the zakat giver has made him rich, which is makruh. The reason is that zakat is meant to meet immediate needs, not to create wealth. The Hanafi school considers this karaha tanzihiyya (slightly disliked).

Key Terms
اغنياء (ighna'): Making a poor person rich by giving him enough zakat to reach the nisab threshold after expenses.
نصاب (nisab): The minimum amount of wealth that makes zakat obligatory; here it refers to the threshold beyond which a person is considered rich.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Whenever the recipient ends up with any amount of money.
B) When the recipient, after paying debts and providing for dependents (less than nisab each), still has a nisab remaining.
C) When the amount given exceeds 200 dirhams.
D) Only when the recipient is not in debt.

Key Takeaway
Giving zakat that leaves a poor person with a nisab after meeting debts and family needs is disliked (makruh tanzih) because zakat is meant to meet basic needs, not to create wealth.

Giving Zakat Based on Taharrī and Its Exception
Ruling on zakat given to presumed recipient - exception for slave/mukatab

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on giving zakat with taharrī and identify the exception of one's own slave or mukatab.

Core Idea
Zakat given with sincere search suffices even if the recipient is later found ineligible — except when given to one's own slave or mukatab.

This lesson addresses a practical scenario every zakat-payer may face: you carefully investigate to find a deserving recipient, give your zakat, and later discover the person was not actually eligible. Does your zakat count? The Hanafi school provides a clear ruling, supported by prophetic guidance, along with a key exception you must know.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its exception
Read the two numbered statements (1201) and (1202). State the general rule: giving with taharrī suffices. Then note the exception: if the recipient is one's own slave or mukatab, it does not suffice.
The exception is based on the principle that the slave's property belongs to the master, and giving to a mukatab ultimately benefits the master.
2
Understand the principle of taharrī
Taharrī means diligent search and effort to determine the correct recipient. The Hanafi school considers this valid even if the assessment turns out wrong, because the giver's obligation is tied to intention and effort, not the actual outcome.
Contrast with the Shafi'i school which requires the recipient to be truly eligible at the time of giving.
3
Analyze the primary proof (hadith)
The hadith of Ma'n bin Yazid: when the father's charity was given to his own son accidentally, the Prophet ruled that each gets what he intended. This shows that intention and effort validate the act, even if the recipient is not the intended one.
Wajh al-dalalah: the Prophet's statement 'you get what you intended' indicates that the validity depends on intention, not actual receipt.
4
Analyze the secondary proof (athar)
The athar of al-Hasan al-Basrī explicitly states that if a man gives zakat to someone he thinks is poor, then it becomes clear he is wealthy, it suffices. This corroborates the Hanafi position.
This is a qawl sahabi (saying of a companion-level scholar) that supports the general rule.
5
Compare with the opposing school
The Shafi'i school holds that the recipient must actually be eligible; if mistaken, the zakat must be re-given. The Hanafi response relies on the hadith and athar, and on the principle that the obligation is discharged by the giver's best effort.
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) is that a Hanafi does not need to repeat the zakat, while a Shafi'i does.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(1201) ولو دفع بتحرٍ لمن ظنّه مصرفا فظهر بخلافه اجزأه (1202) الا ان يكون عبده ومكاتبه. وجه : (1) ان معن بن يزيد حدثه ... و كان ابي يزيد اخرج دنانير يتصدق بها فوضعها عند رجل في المسجد فجئت فاخذتها فاتيته بها فقال والله ما ایاک اردت فخاصمته الی رسول الله فقال لک مانویت یا یزید ولک ما اخذت یا معن (بخاری شریف، باب اذا تصدق علی ابنہ وصولا یشعر ص ۱۹۱ نمبر ۱۴۲۲). (2) عن الحسن في الرجل يعطى زكوته الى فقير ثم يتبين له انه غنى قال اجزى عنه. (مصنف ابن ابی شیبۃ ۹۷ ما قالوافی الرجل یعطی زکوی یعنی وھولا يعلم ج خانی ص ۴۱۳ ، نمبر ۱۰۵۴۳).
(1201) If one gives based on diligent search to someone he assumes is a recipient, and then the opposite appears, it suffices him. (1202) Except if it is his slave or his mukatab. Evidence: (1) Ma'n bin Yazid narrated that his father gave charity and put it with a man in the mosque, then Ma'n took it and brought it back. The father said he did not intend for him, and they went to the Prophet (peace be upon him), who said: 'You get what you intended, O Yazid, and you get what you took, O Ma'n.' (Bukhari). (2) From al-Hasan: concerning a man who gives his zakat to a poor person, then it becomes clear that he is wealthy – he said it suffices him. (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah).
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding a common situation: a person gives zakat to someone they believe is a valid recipient (masrif) through careful investigation (taharrī). If it later turns out the recipient was not eligible, the zakat is still valid according to the Hanafi school. The exception is if the recipient was the giver's own slave or mukatab (a slave contractually earning freedom) – in those cases, the zakat does not suffice because the giver effectively gives to himself (the slave's property belongs to the master) or the money would return to him (in case of mukatab, the money goes to the master). Two proofs are cited: first, the hadith of Ma'n bin Yazid where the Prophet ruled that the father's charity was valid despite not reaching the intended person, indicating that the intention suffices. Second, the athar of al-Hasan al-Basrī explicitly stating that zakat given to someone thought poor who turns out to be rich still fulfills the obligation. The commentary notes that the Hanafi position does not require re-giving.

Key Terms
تحر (taharrī): Diligent search and effort to determine the correct recipient of zakat based on available information.
مصرف (masrif): A valid category for zakat expenditure, i.e., the eight categories mentioned in the Quran (Surah al-Tawbah, 9:60).
مکاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a contract (kitabah) to earn his freedom by paying a specified amount to his master over time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person gives zakat to someone they believe is a poor recipient based on careful investigation, but later discovers the person is actually rich, what is the ruling in the Hanafi school?
A) The zakat is valid and does not need to be re-given.
B) The zakat is invalid and must be re-given.
C) It is makruh but still valid.
D) It is only valid if the giver was absolutely certain.

Key Takeaway
When giving zakat, sincere effort to find a deserving recipient (taharrī) suffices to fulfill the obligation, unless the recipient is your own slave or mukatab.

Ruling on Ighna' in Zakat: The Dislike of Making the Recipient Wealthy
Ruling on makruh tanzihi with evidence from athar and hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on giving zakat that makes the recipient wealthy and identify the condition that triggers the dislike.

Core Idea
Giving zakat so that the recipient exceeds nisab after debts is makruh tanzihi; the Prophet's exception proves the rule.

Zakat is a pillar of Islam meant to purify wealth and help the needy. However, giving so much zakat to a single recipient that he becomes wealthy (ighna') is considered disliked in the Hanafi school. This lesson examines the ruling, the precise condition that activates the dislike, and the evidence from the companions and the Prophet. Understanding this helps zakat payers distribute their zakat in the most meritorious way.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its level
Recognize that ighna' is makruh tanzihi (disliked, not prohibited). The passage states 'wukriha al-ighna''.
Makruh tanzihi means it is better to avoid, but if done, the act is still valid.
2
Analyze the condition for the dislike
The dislike applies only if after paying the recipient's debts and providing for each dependent less than nisab, the recipient is left with a nisab (200 dirhams or equivalent). If the remainder is less than nisab, there is no dislike.
Key point: the condition includes both debts and dependents.
3
Examine the evidence
The proofs include athar (sayings of companions) advising to give below nisab, and a hadith where the Prophet gave 100 camels from sadaqah as blood-money, showing that large amounts are permissible for pressing needs but not the norm.
The hadith illustrates an exception; the general recommendation is to avoid ighna'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(1203) وكره الاغنياء وهو ان يفضل للفقير نصاب بعد قضاء دينه وبعد اعطاء كل فرد من عياله دون نصاب من المدفوع اليه والا فلا يكره. وجه (۱) عن عامر قال اعط من الزكوة ما دون ان يحل على من تعطيه الزكوة (مصنف ابن ابي شيہ نمبر ۱۰۴۳۰). (۲) عن ابي جعفر قال يعطى منها ما بينه و بين المائتين (مصنف ابن ابی شیبۃ ۸۰ ما قالوا فی الزکوة قدر ما یعطی منهاج ثانی ص ۴۰۳، نمبر ۱۰۴۲۹). (۳) ان رجلا من الانصار يقال له سهل بن ابى حثمة اخبره : أن النبي علا الله وداه بـمـائـة مـن ابل الصدقة يعنى دية الانصارى الذى قتل بخیبر (ابوداود شریف، باب کم یعطی الرجل الواحد من الزکوة ؟ ص ۲۴۳، نمبر ۱۶۳۸).
(1203) And it is disliked to make [the poor] wealthy, which is that there remains for the poor person a nisab after paying his debt and after giving each individual of his dependents less than a nisab from what is given to him, otherwise it is not disliked. Evidence: (1) From 'Amir: 'Give from zakat that which does not make the one you give obligatory upon him [i.e., does not make him liable for zakat].' (2) From Abu Ja'far: 'Give from it what is between him and two hundred [dirhams].' (3) The Prophet gave one hundred camels from sadaqah as blood-money for the Ansari killed at Khaybar.
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on making the poor person 'wealthy' (ighna') through zakat. It is considered makruh tanzihi (disliked) to give so much zakat to a single individual that after paying off his debts and providing for each of his dependents an amount below nisab, he is left with a nisab (200 dirhams or equivalent). The underlying reason is that the recipient should remain a needy zakat recipient, not become wealthy enough to owe zakat himself. The evidence includes athar from 'Amir and Abu Ja'far, who advised giving amounts such that the recipient does not reach the zakat threshold (nisab). However, an exception is noted: if there is a pressing need like blood-money (diyah), a larger amount can be given, as the Prophet did when he gave 100 camels from sadaqah as blood-money. The commentary explains that if after providing for dependents the remainder is less than nisab, there is no dislike.

Key Terms
اغناء (ighna'): Making a poor person wealthy by giving so much that he possesses a nisab after basic needs.
نصاب (nisab): The minimum amount of wealth that makes zakat obligatory (200 dirhams of silver or equivalent).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on giving zakat to a poor person in such an amount that after paying his debts and providing for his family, he is left with a nisab?
A) Mustahabb (recommended)
B) Makruh tanzihi (disliked)
C) Haram (forbidden)
D) Wajib (obligatory)

Key Takeaway
It is makruh tanzihi to give zakat that leaves the recipient with a nisab after debts and family needs; the zakat remains valid, but the sunna is to give less than nisab to a single recipient.

Zakat Distribution: Self-Sufficiency and Transfer Dislike
Rulings on zakat amount and transfer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on giving zakat to make the recipient independent and identify the exceptions that remove the dislike of transferring zakat to another city.

Core Idea
Give enough to eliminate need; transfer only for kinship, greater need, piety, or education.

This passage presents two important guidelines for zakat distribution: how much to give and where to send it. The Hanafi school encourages giving an amount that truly frees the recipient from begging, and discourages transferring zakat away from the local area without a valid reason. Understanding these rulings ensures zakat fulfills both its spiritual and social purposes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
Read the passage and separate the two distinct rulings: one about the amount of zakat (mustahabb to give enough for independence), and one about its location (makruh to transfer to another city).
The passage begins with the word 'wa' (and) linking both rulings.
2
Understand the recommended amount
Focus on the phrase 'ighna'uhu 'an al-su'al' (making him independent from asking). This means the giver should give enough to cover the recipient's basic needs for a reasonable period, so they do not need to beg.
The evidence from Ibrahim al-Nakha'i recommends covering all household needs.
3
Examine the dislike of transfer and its exceptions
The phrase 'wa kariha naqlaha' indicates transfer is disliked (makruh tanzihi). But exceptions are listed: relative, more needy, more pious, or beneficial for Muslims through education. If any applies, the dislike is removed.
The presumption is to give locally; exceptions shift the presumption.
4
Analyze the proofs
Two proofs are cited: the practice of Ibrahim, and the hadith of Qabisah which limits permissible begging to basic sustenance. The hadith supports the idea that the giver should provide enough to eliminate the need for further asking.
The hadith uses 'qiwaman min 'aysh' (sustenance of life) to define the amount.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(1204) وندب اغناءه عن السؤال وكره نقلها بعد تمام الحول لبلد آخر لغير قريب و احوج واورع وانفع للمسلمين بتعليم. وجه (۱) عن ابراهيم قال كان يستحب أن يسد بها حاجة اهل البيت (مصنف ابن ابي شيبة ۸۰ ما قالوافي الزكوة قدر ما یعطی منهاج ثانی ص ۴۰۳ نمبر ۱۰۴۲۹). (۲) عن قبيصة بن مخارق الهلالي قال تحملت حمالة ..... و رجل اصابته فاقة حتى يقول ثلاثة من ذوى الحجى من قومه قد اصابت فلانا الفاقة فحلت له المسألة فسأل حتى يصيب قواما من عيش او سدادا من عيش ثم يمسك و ما سواهن من المسألة يا قبيصة ! سحت يأكلها صاحبها سحتا (ابوداود شریف، باب ما تجوز به المسألة ، ص ۲۴۴ نمبر ۱۶۴۰).
(1204) And it is recommended to make him independent from asking, and it is disliked to transfer it after the completion of the year to another city for other than a relative, more needy, more pious, or more beneficial for Muslims through education. Evidence: (1) From Ibrahim: 'It used to be recommended to cover the needs of the household with it.' (2) The hadith of Qabisah ibn Mukhariq al-Hilali: '...until he obtains a sustenance of life or a covering of life, then he refrains. Whatever else is asked is subhat (ill-gotten) for the one who consumes it.'
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage contains two rulings. First, it is mustahabb (recommended) to give zakat in an amount that suffices the recipient's basic needs and makes him independent from begging. The evidence is the practice of Ibrahim (probably Ibrahim al-Nakha'i) who recommended covering all needs of the family. The hadith of Qabisah indicates that asking for charity is only permissible for dire need, enough for sustenance, supporting the idea of giving enough to eliminate need. Second, it is makruh tanzihi to transfer zakat from the locality where it was due to another city after the year has completed, unless the recipient in the other city is a close relative, more needy, more pious, or involved in education that benefits Muslims. The commentary explains that if any of these conditions exist, the transfer is not disliked.

Key Terms
اغناءه عن السؤال (ighna'uhu 'an al-su'al): Giving enough zakat so the recipient no longer needs to beg for basic necessities.
نقل الزكوة (naql al-zakat): Transferring zakat from one city to another after the year has completed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When the recipient in the other city is wealthy
B) When the recipient is a close relative, more needy, more pious, or beneficial for education
C) When the giver has no relatives in his own city
D) It is always disliked, no exceptions

Key Takeaway
Zakat should be given in sufficient amount to eliminate need and should generally be distributed locally, but transfers for kinship, greater need, piety, or education are permissible.
