The Preferred Order of Zakat Recipients and the Prohibition of Transfer
Ruling on preferable zakat recipients and prohibition of transfer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi preferred order of zakat recipients and justify the ruling that transferring zakat to another city is makruh using the dalil from the hadith.

Core Idea
Zakat should be given to the nearest needy relatives first; transferring it to another city is disliked unless local needs are met.

Zakat is a prescribed obligation, but its distribution is not arbitrary. The Hanafi school specifies a preferred order of recipients based on proximity and need, starting with one's own close relatives. This lesson explores that hierarchy and the evidence that restricts transferring zakat to another locality. Understanding this ensures that zakat fulfills its social purpose effectively.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Preferred Order
List the recipients in descending priority: (1) nearest mahram relatives, (2) next of kin, (3) neighbours, (4) people of one's quarter, (5) people of one's craft, (6) people of one's town.
The phrase 'al-aqrab fa al-aqrab' indicates a cascade of proximity.
2
Note the Additional Recommendation
Shaykh Abu Hafs al-Kabir emphasizes that if one has needy relatives, they must be helped first; otherwise the sadaqa is not accepted.
This reinforces the priority of relatives.
3
Analyze the First Proof
The hadith of Mu'adh: 'tu'khadh min aghniya'ihim fa turadd 'ala fuqara'ihim' – zakat should be taken from the rich of a locality and given to its poor, implying it should not be transferred elsewhere.
The imperative 'tu'khadh' and 'turadd' indicate locality-based distribution.
4
Analyze the Remaining Proofs
Umar's practice of returning Bedouin sadaqa to them, the hadith 'ibda' bi man ta'ul' (start with dependents), and the hadith of Qabisah (waiting for local sadaqa) all support keeping zakat within the community.
These proofs further discourage transfer.
5
Derive the Ruling on Transfer
Based on the proofs, transferring zakat to another city is makruh tanzihi (disliked) unless no eligible recipient exists locally. The preferred order is mustahab but not obligatory.
Shafi'is allow transfer more freely; this is a legitimate ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب المصرف
(1205) والافضل صرفها للاقرب فالاقرب من كل ذى رحم محرم منه ثم لجيرانه ثم لاهل محلته ثم لاهل حرفته ثم لاهل بلدته. وقال الشيخ ابو حفص الكبير رحمه الله لا تقبل صدقه الرجل وقرابته محاويج حتى يبدأ بهم فيُسدّ حاجتهم
وجه : (١) عن ابن عباس قال قال رسول الله لمعاذ بن جبل حين بعثه الى اليمن ... قد افترض عليهم صدقة توخذ من اغنيائهم فترد على فقرائهم (٢) سئل عمر عما يؤخذ من صدقات الاعراب كيف يصنع بها فقال عمر: والله لأردن عليهم الصدقة حتى تروح على أحدهم مائة ناقة أو مائة بعير (٣) عن ابي هريرة انه قال : يا رسول الله أى الصدقة أفضل ؟ قال جهد المقل ، و أبدأ بمن تعول (٤) عن قبيصة بن مخارق الهلالي قال تحملت حمالة فأتيت النبي عليه فقال : أقم يا قبيصة ! حتى تأتينا الصدقة فنأمر لك بها
Chapter of Disbursement
(1205) And the best is to give it to the nearest, then the nearest, of every prohibited degree of kinship, then to his neighbors, then to the people of his quarter, then to the people of his craft, then to the people of his town. And Shaykh Abu Hafs al-Kabir (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The sadaqa of a man is not accepted while his relatives are needy until he begins with them and fulfills their need.
Evidence: (1) From Ibn Abbas that the Messenger of Allah said to Mu'adh ibn Jabal when he sent him to Yemen: ... 'Allah has prescribed upon them sadaqa to be taken from their rich and given to their poor.' (2) Umar was asked about what is taken from the sadaqat of the Bedouins: what should be done with it? Umar said: By Allah, I will give the sadaqa back to them so that one of them will return with one hundred she-camels or one hundred camels. (3) From Abu Hurayra that he said: O Messenger of Allah, which sadaqa is best? He said: The effort of the poor person, and start with those you support. (4) From Qabisah ibn Mukhariq al-Hilali who said: I undertook a debt (hamala) and came to the Prophet, and he said: Stay, O Qabisah, until the sadaqa comes to us, and we will order some for you.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage states the ruling on the preferable distribution of zakat. It begins by listing the best recipients in order of priority: first, the nearest relatives who are mahram (unmarriageable kin) – meaning those with whom marriage is permanently prohibited due to blood relationship. Then next of kin in descending degrees of closeness. After relatives, the next priority is neighbors, then residents of one's quarter, then people of one's craft or profession, then residents of one's town. The text then quotes Shaykh Abu Hafs al-Kabir, who emphasizes that if a person has needy relatives, his sadaqa given to others is not accepted until he fulfills their needs. The passage then provides four proofs: the first is the hadith of Mu'adh, where the Prophet instructed that zakat be taken from the rich of a locality and given to its poor, indicating it should not be transferred to another city. The second is the practice of Umar who returned the sadaqa to the Bedouins. The third is the hadith advising to start with dependents. The fourth is the hadith of Qabisah about giving to those in need who come asking. These proofs support the ruling that transferring zakat to another city is makruh (disliked) unless there is no need locally.

Key Terms
مصرف (masrif): The category or recipient of zakat disbursement.
ذى رحم محرم (dhi rahim muhrim): A relative with whom marriage is permanently prohibited due to blood relationship.
جهد المقل (juhd al-muqill): The effort or hardship of a person with limited means.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Poor neighbors
C) People of the same profession
D) All poor Muslims equally

Key Takeaway
Zakat should be given to the nearest mahram relatives first; transferring it to another city is makruh tanzihi unless no local eligible recipients exist.

Conditions for Obligation of Sadaqat al-Fitr
Ruling on the obligation of Zakat al-Fitr and its conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which Zakat al-Fitr becomes wajib in the Hanafi school and identify the dalil that establishes it.

Core Idea
Zakat al-Fitr is wajib for every free Muslim who owns nisab at dawn of Eid, even without the year passing.

Zakat al-Fitr is a unique obligation tied to the completion of Ramadan. Unlike annual zakat on wealth, it does not require the passage of a full lunar year. In this lesson, we examine the specific conditions that trigger this obligation and the evidence from the Sunnah that supports it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The text rules that Sadaqat al-Fitr (Zakat al-Fitr) is wajib (obligatory). Note that Hanafis use 'wajib' not 'fard' because the proof is from ahad hadith.
The ruling is at the beginning of the matn: 'تجب' indicates wujub.
2
List the conditions for obligation
The person must be a free Muslim who owns the nisab (or its value) at dawn of Eid, free from debt and essential needs of self and dependents. The nisab need not be for trade and does not require a full year.
The conditions are stated: حر, مسلم, مالك لنصاب, فارغ عن الدين وحاجته الاصلية.
3
Note the difference from zakat on wealth
Contrast: zakat on wealth requires hawla (a full lunar year). Zakat al-Fitr is due at dawn of Eid regardless of hawla. Also, Zakat al-Fitr is wajib on every eligible person, not just on the wealth itself.
The text explicitly says 'وان لم يحل عليه الحول' (even if the year has not passed).
4
Locate the dalil
Two proofs are cited: (1) A hadith where the Prophet interprets the Qur'anic verse about 'tazakka' as referring to Zakat al-Fitr. (2) The well-known hadith of Ibn Umar stating that the Prophet prescribed Zakat al-Fitr as one sa' of dates or barley on all Muslims.
The first hadith is weak (Kathir ibn Abdullah al-Muzani is disputed), but the Ibn Umar hadith is sahih.
5
Analyse the wajh al-dalalah
The word 'farada' in the Ibn Umar hadith explicitly means 'prescribed as obligatory'. The Hanafis consider it zanni al-thubut (ahad) so it establishes wujub, not the absolute fard of mutawatir texts.
The Shafi'is consider the same hadith as establishing fard because they do not differentiate between fard and wajib on the basis of thubut.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب صدقة الفطر
(1206) تجب على حر مسلم مالك لنصاب او قيمته وان لم يحل عليه الحول عند طلوع فجر يوم الفطر (1207) ولم يكن للتجارة فارغ عن الدين وحاجته الاصلية وحوائج عياله.
وجه : عن كثير بن عبد الله المزني عن ابيه عن جده ان رسول الله سئل عن قوله {قد افلح من تزكى و ذكر اسم ربه فصلى} [الاعلى 14-15] قال : هي زكاة الفطر
و عن ابن عمر قال فرض رسول الله زكوة الفطر صاعا من تمر او صاعا من شعير على العبد والحر والذكر والانثى والصغير والكبير من المسلمين وامر بها ان تودى قبل خروج الناس الى الصلوة
Chapter of Zakat al-Fitr
(1206) It is wajib upon a free Muslim who owns the nisab or its value, even if the year has not passed upon it, at the time of dawn of the day of Fitr (Eid). (1207) And it is not for trade, free from debt and his basic needs and the needs of his dependents.
Evidence: From Kathir ibn Abdullah al-Muzani from his father from his grandfather that the Messenger of Allah was asked about His saying {Successful is he who purifies himself and mentions the name of his Lord and prays} [al-A'la 14-15]; he said: It is Zakat al-Fitr.
And from Ibn Umar: The Messenger of Allah prescribed Zakat al-Fitr as one sa' of dates or one sa' of barley upon the slave and free, male and female, young and old among the Muslims, and commanded that it be given before the people go out to the prayer.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter on Sadaqat al-Fitr (also called Zakat al-Fitr). It begins by stating the ruling: it is wajib (obligatory) upon a free Muslim who possesses the nisab (minimum threshold of wealth) or its value, even if a full lunar year has not passed – unlike zakat on wealth which requires the passing of a year. The obligation becomes due at the dawn of Eid al-Fitr (the day after Ramadan). The text then adds conditions: the nisab must be free from debt and from essential needs (like primary residence, basic clothing, etc.) and the needs of one's dependents. The nisab does not need to be for trade (it can be any surplus wealth). The passage provides two proofs: first, a hadith from the Prophet interpreting the Qur'anic verse about 'tazakka' as referring to Zakat al-Fitr. Second, the famous hadith of Ibn Umar that the Prophet made Zakat al-Fitr obligatory as one sa' of staple food on all Muslims, free or slave, male or female, young or old, and ordered it to be given before the Eid prayer.

Key Terms
صدقة الفطر (sadaqat al-fitr): A charitable due given at the end of Ramadan, on Eid al-Fitr, also called Zakat al-Fitr.
نصاب (nisab): The minimum amount of wealth that triggers obligation; here, the value of 200 dirhams of silver or equivalent.
حول (hawl): A full lunar year of ownership, which is a condition for zakat on wealth but not for Zakat al-Fitr.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When does Zakat al-Fitr become wajib according to the Hanafi school?
A) At the beginning of Ramadan
B) At dawn on the day of Eid al-Fitr
C) After a full year of owning the nisab
D) Only if one has extra food for a day

Key Takeaway
Zakat al-Fitr is wajib upon every free Muslim who owns nisab at dawn of Eid, with conditions of being free from debt and basic needs, based on the Prophet's command.

Obligation and Conditions of Sadaqat al-Fitr
Stating the obligation and conditions for Sadaqat al-Fitr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling (wajib) of Sadaqat al-Fitr, identify its three conditions of obligation, and explain the proof from the hadith of Ibn Umar including the Hanafi classification of the hadith as ahad.

Core Idea
Sadaqat al-Fitr is wajib at dawn of Eid, without hawl, upon every free Muslim with surplus beyond needs.

Sadaqat al-Fitr is a unique charity that differs from regular Zakat in its timing and conditions. In this lesson, we examine its obligation as derived from the hadith of Ibn Umar, the three conditions of obligation (freedom, Islam, nisab), and the proof structure that leads the Hanafis to classify it as wajib rather than fard.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? What is the hukm level according to the Hanafi school? Look for the word 'wajib' in the text. The text says 'تجب' (it is obligatory).
The text classifies it as wajib, not fard.
2
Locate the three conditions
The text lists three conditions: being free, Muslim, and owner of nisab. Note that hawl (year) is not a condition.
These conditions are explicitly stated in the commentary.
3
Examine the dalil
The key proof is the hadith of Ibn Umar with the word 'farada'. Identify the type of hadith (ahad) and how it establishes wujub.
The Hanafi principle: al-amr yufidu al-wujub, but since ahad, it is wajib not fard.
4
Understand the Hanafi classification
Why does the Hanafi school classify this as wajib rather than fard? The answer lies in the epistemological status of the hadith (zanni al-thubut).
Compare with other schools that consider it fard.
5
Apply to a scenario
Consider someone who has nisab but has not passed a full year on it. At dawn of Eid, is Sadaqat al-Fitr obligatory? Yes, because hawl is not a condition.
This distinguishes it from regular Zakat.

Chapter of Sadaqat al-Fitr. (1206) It is obligatory upon a free Muslim who owns a nisab or its value, even if the year has not passed over it, at the time of dawn on the day of Fitr. (1207) And it is not for trade, free from debt, and free from his basic need and the needs of his dependents. Explanation of Sadaqat al-Fitr. Essential note: The charity given on the day of Eid is called Sadaqat al-Fitr. Because after fasting the entire month of Ramadan, that day is the day of breaking the fast, so it is called Sadaqat al-Fitr. Its proof is this verse: from Kathir ibn Abdillah al-Muzani from his father from his grandfather that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) was asked about His saying: 'Indeed successful is he who purifies himself and mentions the name of his Lord and prays' [Surah al-A'la 87:14-15]. He said: 'It is Zakat al-Fitr.' (Sunan al-Bayhaqi, chapter compilation of chapters of Zakat al-Fitr, vol. 4, p. 268, no. 7668). In this hadith it is mentioned that this verse refers to Sadaqat al-Fitr. Translation: (1206) Sadaqat al-Fitr is obligatory upon a free Muslim who owns a nisab or its value, even if the year has not completed upon him at the time of dawn on the day of Eid al-Fitr. Explanation: There are three conditions for Sadaqat al-Fitr to be obligatory: [1] Free; not obligatory upon a slave. [2] Muslim; not obligatory upon a disbeliever. [3] Owner of a nisab or its value, even if the year has not passed, then at the morning of Eid al-Fitr, Sadaqat al-Fitr becomes obligatory. Proof: (1) The proof that Sadaqat al-Fitr is obligatory is this hadith: Ibn Umar said: 'The Messenger of Allah made obligatory Zakat al-Fitr, a sa' of dates or a sa' of barley, upon the slave and the free, the male and the female, the young and the old among the Muslims, and he ordered that it be given before the people go out to the prayer.' (Bukhari, chapter of obligation of Sadaqat al-Fitr, p. 204, no. 1503; Muslim, chapter of Zakat al-Fitr upon Muslims from dates and barley, p. 395, no. 2278/984). In this hadith, from the word 'farada', the Hanafis consider giving Sadaqat al-Fitr as obligatory (wajib). And the remaining proof has passed in the Book of Zakat. Translation: (1207) Even if it is not for trade, free from debt, and free from basic need, and from the need of his dependents. Explanation: It is not necessary that this nisab be for trade, and it is not necessary that it be productive (growing), and it is free from basic need. (1208) And what is considered in it is sufficiency, not estimation. It is his dwelling, his furniture, his clothes, his horse, his weapon, and his slaves for service. (1209) He gives it on behalf of himself and his poor young children, and if they are rich, he gives it from their wealth. (And also free from the basic need of those who are under his support). Then Sadaqat al-Fitr becomes obligatory. Proof: And the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'No charity except from a person of means (ghina).' (Bukhari, chapter of interpretation of the saying 'after a bequest they bequeath or a debt', p. 454, no. 2750). In this hadith, it is that when one is wealthy, then Sadaqat al-Fitr is obligatory. Translation: (1208) And in need, consideration is given to sufficiency, not to imposing from oneself. That is the house, the house's furniture, and clothes, and the horse, and the weapon, and the service slaves. Explanation: In need, it is not that one imposes something from oneself, rather the need is for food for the year, and also for the food of dependents. A house to live in, furniture in the house, clothes, a horse, a weapon, and a slave for service. These are things of a person's need. If there is extra beyond all these, then it will be said that he is free from basic need. (3) The detail of basic need is known from this athar: from Sa'id ibn Jubayr: 'Zakah is given to one who has a house, a servant, and a horse.' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, no. 75: 'Whoever has a house and a servant is given from zakat', vol. 7, p. 402, no. 10415). From this athar it is understood that whoever has a house to live in, a slave for service, and a horse for jihad is not considered poor. Translation: (1209) He will give Sadaqat al-Fitr on behalf of himself and on behalf of his poor young children. If the children are rich, he will give it from their wealth. Explanation: He will give Sadaqat al-Fitr on his own behalf and on behalf of his young children. If the young children have their own wealth, then he will give from their wealth, because the original is that it is obligatory upon the young child himself, but because he has no wealth, the father gives. But if the child has wealth, then it is obligatory upon the child himself. Proof: (1) In this hadith, it is that he gives on his own behalf, and it indicates that he gives on behalf of his young children. 'Ibn Umar said: The Messenger of Allah made obligatory Zakat al-Fitr, a sa' of dates or a sa' of barley, upon the slave and the free, the male and the female, the young and the old among the Muslims, and he ordered that it be given before the people go out to prayer.' (Bukhari, no. 1503; Muslim, no. 2278/984). In this hadith, it is that Sadaqat al-Fitr is given on behalf of all males and females. (2) In this hadith, it is that it is given on behalf of young children. 'Ibn Umar said: The Messenger of Allah ordered Sadaqat al-Fitr on behalf of the young and the old, the free and the slave, from those whom you support.' (Dar
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive ruling on Sadaqat al-Fitr (Zakat al-Fitr), presented in the form of a matn with accompanying commentary (sharh). The author begins by stating the obligation upon a free Muslim who owns a nisab (minimum wealth threshold) at dawn of Eid al-Fitr, even if a full lunar year has not passed—unlike regular Zakat where a year (hawl) is required. The obligation is not dependent on the wealth being for trade (tijarah) or productive (nami); it is sufficient that it exceeds basic needs (hajah asliyyah) and debts. The author then explains the conditions: freedom, Islam, and ownership of nisab. Proof is provided from the hadith of Ibn Umar where the Prophet (peace be upon him) 'farada' (made obligatory) Sadaqat al-Fitr. The Hanafi school interprets 'farada' as establishing wajib, not fard in the technical sense of necessary belief, but an obligation established by a hadith that is zanni al-thubut. Further, the text clarifies that the nisab need not be for trade and that basic needs include dwelling, furniture, clothing, horse, weapon, and service slaves—these are considered 'kifayah' (sufficiency), not a fixed estimation. Finally, the ruling states that a person pays for himself and his poor young children; if the children have wealth, the father pays from that wealth. The proofs rely on the same hadith and a narration from Sa'id ibn Jubayr indicating that possession of a house, servant, and horse does not preclude one from receiving zakat, but for Sadaqat al-Fitr, such items are considered basic needs and are exempt from the nisab calculation.

Key Terms
صدقة الفطر (ṣadaqat al-fiṭr): The charity given on the day of Eid al-Fitr, obligatory upon those who possess surplus wealth beyond basic needs.
نصاب (niṣāb): The minimum amount of wealth that triggers the obligation of Sadaqat al-Fitr; it need not be productive or for trade, only surplus beyond basic needs and debts.
حاجت اصلیہ (ḥājat aṣliyyah): Basic needs that are exempt from the calculation of nisab: dwelling, furniture, clothing, horse, weapon, and service slaves.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is the correct legal classification of Sadaqat al-Fitr in the Hanafi school?
A) Fard because the hadith says 'farada'
B) Wajib because the hadith is ahad
C) Sunnah because it is voluntary
D) Mustahabb because it is not mentioned in the Quran

Key Takeaway
Sadaqat al-Fitr is wajib at dawn of Eid upon every free Muslim who has surplus beyond basic needs, proven by the hadith of Ibn Umar, and its obligation is lower than fard due to zanni thubut.

The Criterion for Sadaqat al-Fitr: Sufficiency Not Estimation
Defining the wealth criterion for Sadaqat al-Fitr obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi criterion for obligation of Sadaqat al-Fitr and explain why it is based on sufficiency (kifaya) rather than a fixed nisab (taqdir), using the evidence from the passage.

Core Idea
Sadaqat al-Fitr is wajib only when you have surplus beyond basic needs—no fixed nisab required.

This passage from the section on Sadaqat al-Fitr addresses a key point unique to the Hanafi school: the threshold for obligation. Unlike Zakat al-Mal, which requires a specific nisab, Sadaqat al-Fitr is obligatory when one has enough to cover essential needs and something extra. The author explains this using a narration from a Companion, highlighting that basic necessities like a house, furniture, clothing, a horse, a weapon, and a household servant are not counted as wealth.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling and its criterion
Identify the Hanafi position: Sadaqat al-Fitr is wajib upon anyone who has surplus beyond basic needs (kifaya), not a fixed amount (taqdir).
The text says: 'والمعتبر فيها الكفاية لا التقدير'.
2
Identify the basic needs listed
What items are considered essential and thus exempt from counting as wealth? The passage lists: dwelling, furniture, clothes, horse, weapon, and a servant.
These are the minimum necessities for a person's livelihood.
3
Examine the evidence (athar)
The athar from Sa'id ibn Jubayr states that a person with a house, servant, and horse can receive zakat, implying he is not wealthy. This supports that such items are not part of surplus wealth.
The athar is from Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah and is used as proof for the criterion.
4
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical difference between the Hanafi criterion and other schools that use nisab? A person with only basic necessities but no extra cash is not obligated in the Hanafi school, whereas some other schools might obligate him if he has a certain amount of cash.
This difference affects many people who are asset-rich but cash-poor.

(١٢٠٨) والمعتبر فيها الكفاية لا التقدير وهى مسكنه واثاثه وثيابه وفرسه وسلاحه وعبيده للخدمة
ترجمه : (۱۲۰۸) اور ضرورت میں اعتبار کافی ہونے کا ہے اپنی طرف سے فرض کر لینا نہیں ہے مکان ، اور مکان کا سامان ، اور کپڑے، اور گھوڑا ، اور ہتھیار ، اور خدمت کے غلام ہیں۔
تشریح : ضرورت میں یہ نہیں ہے کہ اپنی طرف سے کچھ فرض کر لے، بلکہ ضرورت یہ ہے کہ سال بھر کے کھانے کا ہو، اور عیال کے کھانے کا بھی ہو۔ رہنے کے لئے مکان ہو ۔ مکان میں سامان ہو، کپڑے ہوں ، گھوڑا ہو، ہتھیار ہو، اور خدمت کرنے کے لئے ایک غلام ہو، یہ آدمی کی ضرورت کی چیز ہیں، ان سب سے زیادہ ہو تو کہا جائے گا کہ حاجت اصلیہ سے فارغ ہے۔ (۳) حاجت اصلیہ کی تفصیل کا پتہ اس اثر سے ہوتا ہے ۔ عن سعيد بن جبيـر قـال يـعـطـى من الزكوة من لـه الدار والخادم والفرس . (مصنف ابن ابی هیه ۷۵ من له دار و خادم يعطى من الزكوة ج خانی ص ۴۰۲ نمبر ۱۰۴۱۵) اس اثر سے معلوم ہوا کہ جس کے پاس رہنے کا گھر ہو اور خدمت کا غلام ہو اور جہاد کا گھوڑا ہو وہ منی نہیں ہے۔
Chapter of Sadaqat al-Fitr. (1208) And the criterion in it is sufficiency, not estimation, and they are his dwelling, his furniture, his clothes, his horse, his weapon, and his slaves for service. Translation: (1208) And the criterion in need is sufficiency, not self-imposed estimation: house, house items, clothes, horse, weapon, and slaves for service. Explanation: In need, it is not that one imposes something from himself; rather, need is that he has food for a year, and food for his dependents. He has a house to live in, furniture in the house, clothes, a horse, a weapon, and a slave for service. These are a person's necessities. If he has more than all these, it will be said that he is free from basic needs. (3) The detail of basic needs is known from this athar: From Sa'id ibn Jubayr, he said: 'He is given from zakat who has a house, a servant, and a horse.' (Musannaf Ibn Abi Shaybah, 75: from him who has a house and a servant is given from zakat, Khasani p. 402, no. 10415) From this athar it is known that whoever has a house to live in, a servant for service, and a horse for jihad is not wealthy.
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage establishes the foundational criterion for Sadaqat al-Fitr obligation. The matn states that the criterion is kifaya (sufficiency) not taqdir (estimation). That is, one does not need to have a fixed amount of wealth; rather, one must have surplus beyond basic necessities. The commentator (sharh) then enumerates basic needs: dwelling, furniture, clothes, horse, weapon, and a slave for service. These are considered essential for a person. The proof is an athar from Sa'id ibn Jubayr, which is a narration from the Companion that a person with a house, servant, and horse can still receive zakat, implying he is not wealthy. This indicates that such items are not counted as wealth for the purpose of obligation. The ruling is that Sadaqat al-Fitr is obligatory only when one possesses extra wealth beyond these essentials.

Key Terms
صدقة الفطر (ṣadaqat al-fiṭr): The obligatory charity given at the end of Ramadan, incumbent upon every free Muslim who has surplus beyond basic needs.
كفاية (kifāyah): Sufficiency; having enough wealth to cover one's basic needs for a year including dependents.
تقدير (taqdīr): Estimation; a fixed amount of wealth as a threshold.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the criterion for being obligated to pay Sadaqat al-Fitr?
A) Having a fixed amount of money (nisab)
B) Having surplus beyond basic needs (kifaya)
C) Being a free adult Muslim
D) Having a house and a horse

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uniquely holds that Sadaqat al-Fitr is wajib only when one has surplus wealth beyond basic necessities, not upon reaching a fixed nisab.
